<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Moon_transit_of_sun_large.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A lunar transit of the Sun captured during calibration of STEREO B's ultraviolet imaging cameras<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[172]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first satellites designed to observe the Sun were <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_program" title="Pioneer program">Pioneers</a> 5, 6, 7, 8 and 9, which were launched between 1959 and 1968. These probes orbited the Sun at a distance similar to that of Earth, and made the first detailed measurements of the solar wind and the solar magnetic field. <a href="/wiki/Pioneer_9" class="mw-redirect" title="Pioneer 9">Pioneer 9</a> operated for a particularly long time, transmitting data until May 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[173]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[174]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s, two <a href="/wiki/Helios_probes" class="mw-redirect" title="Helios probes">Helios</a> spacecraft and the <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> <a href="/wiki/Apollo_Telescope_Mount" title="Apollo Telescope Mount">Apollo Telescope Mount</a> provided scientists with significant new data on solar wind and the solar corona. The Helios 1 and 2 probes were U.S.–German collaborations that studied the solar wind from an orbit carrying the spacecraft inside <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>'s orbit at <a href="/wiki/Perihelion" class="mw-redirect" title="Perihelion">perihelion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burlaga2001_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burlaga2001-181">[175]</a></sup> The Skylab space station, launched by NASA in 1973, included a solar <a href="/wiki/Observatory" title="Observatory">observatory</a> module called the Apollo Telescope Mount that was operated by astronauts resident on the station.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi2006_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-94">[89]</a></sup> Skylab made the first time-resolved observations of the solar transition region and of ultraviolet emissions from the solar corona.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi2006_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-94">[89]</a></sup> Discoveries included the first observations of <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejections</a>, then called "coronal transients", and of <a href="/wiki/Coronal_hole" title="Coronal hole">coronal holes</a>, now known to be intimately associated with the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Burlaga2001_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Burlaga2001-181">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1980, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Maximum_Mission" title="Solar Maximum Mission">Solar Maximum Mission</a> was launched by <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>. This spacecraft was designed to observe <a href="/wiki/Gamma_ray" title="Gamma ray">gamma rays</a>, <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> radiation from <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> during a time of high solar activity and <a href="/wiki/Sun#External_links" title="Sun">solar luminosity</a>. Just a few months after launch, however, an electronics failure caused the probe to go into standby mode, and it spent the next three years in this inactive state. In 1984 <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger" title="Space Shuttle Challenger">Space Shuttle <i>Challenger</i></a> mission <a href="/wiki/STS-41C" class="mw-redirect" title="STS-41C">STS-41C</a> retrieved the satellite and repaired its electronics before re-releasing it into orbit. The Solar Maximum Mission subsequently acquired thousands of images of the solar corona before <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_reentry" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric reentry">re-entering</a> Earth's atmosphere in June 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Launched in 1991, Japan's <a href="/wiki/Yohkoh" title="Yohkoh">Yohkoh</a> (<i>Sunbeam</i>) satellite observed solar flares at X-ray wavelengths. Mission data allowed scientists to identify several different types of flares, and demonstrated that the corona away from regions of peak activity was much more dynamic and active than had previously been supposed. Yohkoh observed an entire solar cycle but went into standby mode when an <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">annular eclipse</a> in 2001 caused it to lose its lock on the Sun. It was destroyed by atmospheric re-entry in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the most important solar missions to date has been the <a href="/wiki/Solar_and_Heliospheric_Observatory" title="Solar and Heliospheric Observatory">Solar and Heliospheric Observatory</a>, jointly built by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> and <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> and launched on 2 December 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi2006_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-94">[89]</a></sup> Originally intended to serve a two-year mission, a mission extension through 2012 was approved in October 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-sohoext_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sohoext-184">[178]</a></sup> It has proven so useful that a follow-on mission, the <a href="/wiki/Solar_Dynamics_Observatory" title="Solar Dynamics Observatory">Solar Dynamics Observatory</a> (SDO), was launched in February 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-sdolaunch_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sdolaunch-185">[179]</a></sup> Situated at the <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point" title="Lagrangian point">Lagrangian point</a> between Earth and the Sun (at which the gravitational pull from both is equal), SOHO has provided a constant view of the Sun at many wavelengths since its launch.<sup id="cite_ref-Dwivedi2006_94-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dwivedi2006-94">[89]</a></sup> Besides its direct solar observation, SOHO has enabled the discovery of a large number of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a>, mostly tiny <a href="/wiki/Sungrazing_comet" title="Sungrazing comet">sungrazing comets</a> that incinerate as they pass the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[180]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg/300px-Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg/450px-Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg/600px-Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1080" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Giant_prominence_on_the_sun_erupted.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A solar prominence erupts in August 2012, as captured by SDO</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>All these satellites have observed the Sun from the plane of the ecliptic, and so have only observed its equatorial regions in detail. The <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_probe" class="mw-redirect" title="Ulysses probe">Ulysses probe</a> was launched in 1990 to study the Sun's polar regions. It first travelled to <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, to "slingshot" into an orbit that would take it far above the plane of the ecliptic. Once Ulysses was in its scheduled orbit, it began observing the solar wind and magnetic field strength at high solar latitudes, finding that the solar wind from high latitudes was moving at about 750&#160;km/s, which was slower than expected, and that there were large magnetic waves emerging from high latitudes that scattered galactic <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elemental abundances in the photosphere are well known from <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_spectroscopy" title="Astronomical spectroscopy">spectroscopic</a> studies, but the composition of the interior of the Sun is more poorly understood. A <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> sample return mission, <a href="/wiki/Genesis_(spacecraft)" title="Genesis (spacecraft)">Genesis</a>, was designed to allow astronomers to directly measure the composition of solar material.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[182]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/STEREO" title="STEREO">Solar Terrestrial Relations Observatory</a> (STEREO) mission was launched in October 2006. Two identical spacecraft were launched into orbits that cause them to (respectively) pull further ahead of and fall gradually behind Earth. This enables <a href="/wiki/Stereoscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Stereoscopic">stereoscopic</a> imaging of the Sun and solar phenomena, such as <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejections" class="mw-redirect" title="Coronal mass ejections">coronal mass ejections</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-inst_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inst-189">[183]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Indian_Space_Research_Organisation" title="Indian Space Research Organisation">Indian Space Research Organisation</a> has scheduled the launch of a 100&#160;kg satellite named <a href="/wiki/Aditya_(spacecraft)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aditya (spacecraft)">Aditya</a> for 2017–18. Its main instrument will be a <a href="/wiki/Coronagraph" title="Coronagraph">coronagraph</a> for studying the dynamics of the Solar corona.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[185]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Observation_and_effects">Observation and effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg/300px-Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg" width="300" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg/450px-Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg/600px-Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4228" data-file-height="2525" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anatomy_of_a_Sunset-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During certain atmospheric conditions, the Sun becomes clearly visible to the naked eye, and can be observed without stress to the eyes. Click on this photo to see the full cycle of a <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">sunset</a>, as observed from the high plains of the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg/220px-STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg/330px-STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg/440px-STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Russian_Orbital_Segment.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Sun, as seen from low Earth orbit overlooking the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>. This sunlight is not filtered by the lower atmosphere, which blocks much of the solar spectrum</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The brightness of the Sun can cause pain from looking at it with the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a>; however, doing so for brief periods is not hazardous for normal non-dilated eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[187]</a></sup> Looking directly at the Sun causes <a href="/wiki/Phosphene" title="Phosphene">phosphene</a> visual artifacts and temporary partial blindness. It also delivers about 4&#160;milliwatts of sunlight to the retina, slightly heating it and potentially causing damage in eyes that cannot respond properly to the brightness.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[188]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[189]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">UV</a> exposure gradually yellows the lens of the eye over a period of years, and is thought to contribute to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Cataracts" class="mw-redirect" title="Cataracts">cataracts</a>, but this depends on general exposure to solar UV, and not whether one looks directly at the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[190]</a></sup> Long-duration viewing of the direct Sun with the naked eye can begin to cause UV-induced, sunburn-like lesions on the retina after about 100 seconds, particularly under conditions where the UV light from the Sun is intense and well focused;<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[191]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[192]</a></sup> conditions are worsened by young eyes or new lens implants (which admit more UV than aging natural eyes), Sun angles near the zenith, and observing locations at high altitude.</p>
<p>Viewing the Sun through light-concentrating <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Binoculars" title="Binoculars">binoculars</a> may result in permanent damage to the retina without an appropriate filter that blocks UV and substantially dims the sunlight. When using an attenuating filter to view the Sun, the viewer is cautioned to use a filter specifically designed for that use. Some improvised filters that pass UV or <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">IR</a> rays, can actually harm the eye at high brightness levels.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[193]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herschel_wedge" title="Herschel wedge">Herschel wedges</a>, also called Solar Diagonals, are effective and inexpensive for small telescopes. The sunlight that is destined for the eyepiece is reflected from an unsilvered surface of a piece of glass. Only a very small fraction of the incident light is reflected. The rest passes through the glass and leaves the instrument. If the glass breaks because of the heat, no light at all is reflected, making the device fail-safe. Simple filters made of darkened glass allow the full intensity of sunlight to pass through if they break, endangering the observer's eyesight. Unfiltered binoculars can deliver hundreds of times as much energy as using the naked eye, possibly causing immediate damage. It is claimed that even brief glances at the midday Sun through an unfiltered telescope can cause permanent damage.<sup id="cite_ref-Macdonald_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Macdonald-200">[194]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg/220px-Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg/330px-Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg/440px-Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Doppelsonne_Halo_Echzell_Hessen_12-08-2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Halo_(optical_phenomenon)" title="Halo (optical phenomenon)">Halo</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sun_dog" title="Sun dog">sun dogs</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Partial <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar eclipses</a> are hazardous to view because the eye's <a href="/wiki/Pupil" title="Pupil">pupil</a> is not adapted to the unusually high visual contrast: the pupil dilates according to the total amount of light in the field of view, <i>not</i> by the brightest object in the field. During partial eclipses most sunlight is blocked by the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> passing in front of the Sun, but the uncovered parts of the photosphere have the same <a href="/wiki/Surface_brightness" title="Surface brightness">surface brightness</a> as during a normal day. In the overall gloom, the pupil expands from ~2&#160;mm to ~6&#160;mm, and each retinal cell exposed to the solar image receives up to ten times more light than it would looking at the non-eclipsed Sun. This can damage or kill those cells, resulting in small permanent blind spots for the viewer.<sup id="cite_ref-Espenak_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Espenak-201">[195]</a></sup> The hazard is insidious for inexperienced observers and for children, because there is no perception of pain: it is not immediately obvious that one's vision is being destroyed.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Actual_Sunrise.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Actual_Sunrise.jpeg/300px-Actual_Sunrise.jpeg" width="300" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Actual_Sunrise.jpeg/450px-Actual_Sunrise.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Actual_Sunrise.jpeg/600px-Actual_Sunrise.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="1316" data-file-height="884" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Actual_Sunrise.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A sunrise</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Sunrise" title="Sunrise">sunrise</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">sunset</a>, sunlight is attenuated because of <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">Rayleigh scattering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mie_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Mie theory">Mie scattering</a> from a particularly long passage through Earth's atmosphere,<sup id="cite_ref-Haber2005_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haber2005-202">[196]</a></sup> and the Sun is sometimes faint enough to be viewed comfortably with the naked eye or safely with optics (provided there is no risk of bright sunlight suddenly appearing through a break between clouds). Hazy conditions, atmospheric dust, and high humidity contribute to this atmospheric attenuation.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[197]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a href="/wiki/Optical_phenomenon" class="mw-redirect" title="Optical phenomenon">optical phenomenon</a>, known as a <a href="/wiki/Green_flash" title="Green flash">green flash</a>, can sometimes be seen shortly after sunset or before sunrise. The flash is caused by light from the Sun just below the horizon being <a href="/wiki/Refraction" title="Refraction">bent</a> (usually through a <a href="/wiki/Temperature_inversion" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperature inversion">temperature inversion</a>) towards the observer. Light of shorter wavelengths (violet, blue, green) is bent more than that of longer wavelengths (yellow, orange, red) but the violet and blue light is <a href="/wiki/Rayleigh_scattering" title="Rayleigh scattering">scattered</a> more, leaving light that is perceived as green.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> light from the Sun has <a href="/wiki/Antiseptic" title="Antiseptic">antiseptic</a> properties and can be used to sanitize tools and water. It also causes <a href="/wiki/Sunburn" title="Sunburn">sunburn</a>, and has other biological effects such as the production of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_D" title="Vitamin D">vitamin D</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sun_tanning" title="Sun tanning">sun tanning</a>. Ultraviolet light is strongly attenuated by Earth's <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>, so that the amount of UV varies greatly with <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitude</a> and has been partially responsible for many biological adaptations, including variations in <a href="/wiki/Human_skin_color" title="Human skin color">human skin color</a> in different regions of the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[199]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_System">Planetary System</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a></div>
<p>The Sun has eight known planets. This includes four <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planets" class="mw-redirect" title="Terrestrial planets">terrestrial planets</a> (<a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>), two <a href="/wiki/Gas_giants" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas giants">gas giants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>), and two <a href="/wiki/Ice_giants" class="mw-redirect" title="Ice giants">ice giants</a> (<a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>). The Solar System also has five <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planets" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwarf planets">dwarf planets</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a>, numerous <a href="/wiki/Comets" class="mw-redirect" title="Comets">comets</a>, and a large number of icy bodies which lie beyond the orbit of Neptune.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-short-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-short_18-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-short_18-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-short_18-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">All numbers in this article are short scale. One billion is 10<sup>9</sup>, or 1,000,000,000.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-heavy_elements-32"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-heavy_elements_32-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomical sciences</a>, the term <i>heavy elements</i> (or <i>metals</i>) refers to all <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> except hydrogen and helium.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-38"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-38">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_vent_communities" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrothermal vent communities">Hydrothermal vent communities</a> live so deep under the sea that they have no access to sunlight. Bacteria instead use sulfur compounds as an energy source, via <a href="/wiki/Chemosynthesis" title="Chemosynthesis">chemosynthesis</a>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-56"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-56">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">1.88&#160;Gcd/m<sup>2</sup> is calculated from the solar illuminance of <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7005128000000000000♠</span>128<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span>&#160;lux</span> (see <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">sunlight</a>) times the square of the distance to the center of the Sun, divided by the cross sectional area of the Sun. 1.44&#160;Gcd/m<sup>2</sup> is calculated using <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7004980000000000000♠</span>98<span style="margin-left:.25em;">000</span>&#160;lux</span>.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-power_production_density-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-power_production_density_79-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A 50 kg adult human has a volume of about 0.05 m<sup>3</sup>, which corresponds to 13.8 watts, at the volumetric power of the solar center. This is 285 kcal/day, about 10% of the actual average caloric intake and output for humans in non-stressful conditions.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-particle_density-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-particle_density_95-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Earth's atmosphere near sea level has a particle density of about 2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">25</span></sup>&#160;m<sup>−3</sup>.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-Macdonald-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Macdonald_200-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Macdonald, Lee (2012). "2. Equipment for Observing the Sun". <i>How to Observe the Sun Safely</i>. New York: Springer Science + Business Media. p.&#160;17. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1007%2F978-1-4614-3825-0_2">10.1007/978-1-4614-3825-0_2</a>. <q>NEVER LOOK DIRECTLY AT THE SUN THROUGH ANY FORM OF OPTICAL EQUIPMENT, EVEN FOR AN INSTANT. A brief glimpse of the Sun through a telescope is enough to cause permanent eye damage, or even blindness. Even looking at the Sun with the naked eye for more than a second or two is not safe. Do not assume that it is safe to look at the Sun through a filter, no matter how dark the filter appears to be.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASun&amp;rft.atitle=2.+Equipment+for+Observing+the+Sun&amp;rft.aufirst=Lee&amp;rft.aulast=Macdonald&amp;rft.btitle=How+to+Observe+the+Sun+Safely&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1007%2F978-1-4614-3825-0_2&amp;rft.pages=17&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.pub=Springer+Science+%2B+Business+Media&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-204"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-204">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081216135504/http://www.bbc.co.uk/weather/features/understanding/greenflash.shtml">"The Green Flash"</a>. <li id="cite_note-205"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-205">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFBarsh2003" class="citation journal">Barsh, G.S. <th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="padding-left:0;padding-right:0;width:6.85em;line-height:0.7em;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.75em;"><small><a href="/wiki/G-type_main-sequence_star" title="G-type main-sequence star">G-type</a></small></div>
</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px;width:auto;">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><strong class="selflink">Sun (<b>0 ly</b>)</strong></li>
<li><small><abbr title="8 planets; over 700,000 minor planets (including 5 dwarf planets); over 5000 comets; 173 moons of planets; over 200 moons of minor planets (including 8 moons of dwarf planets); bodies of planetary rings (mostly were observed rings, not separate bodies, but over 150 moonlets were observed); numerous small bodies, observed as meteors and meteorites, but not catalogued as minor planets or comets; spacecraft; space debris; cosmonauts. Some known comets and spacecrafts, currently located within Solar System boundaries, are not gravitationally bound with the Sun, and therefore formally are separate (interstellar) objects.">rest</abbr> of <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a></small></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">α Cen (<b>4.366</b><span style="margin-left:0.3em;margin-right:0.15em">±</span>0.007 <b>ly</b>)</a></li>
<li><small>K-type main-sequence star</small><small>&#160;B</small></li>
<li><small>red dwarf</small><small>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">C (Proxima Centauri) (<b>4.2430</b> ± 0.0017 <b>ly</b>)</a></small></li>
<li><small>planet</small><small>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri_b" title="Proxima Centauri b">b</a></small></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
</td>
</tr>
<tr style="height:2px">
<td colspan="2"></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="navbox-abovebelow" colspan="2">
<div><small><i>Italic</i> are systems without known <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_parallax" class="mw-redirect" title="Trigonometric parallax">trigonometric parallax</a>.</small></div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
